            function createLink(src) {
                if ("connection" in navigator) {
                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {
                        var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");
                        preloadLink.rel = "preload";
                        preloadLink.href = src;
                        preloadLink.as = "font";
                        preloadLink.type = "font/woff2";
                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

        </script>

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome of the red alga Porphyridium purpureum"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2013 4:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2013-06-17"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2013 Nature Publishing Group, a division of Macmillan Publishers Limited. All Rights Reserved."/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The limited knowledge we have about red algal genomes comes from the highly specialized extremophiles, Cyanidiophyceae. Here, we describe the first genome sequence from a mesophilic, unicellular red alga, Porphyridium purpureum. The 8,355 predicted genes in P. purpureum, hundreds of which are likely to be implicated in a history of horizontal gene transfer, reside in a genome of 19.7&#8201;Mbp with 235 spliceosomal introns. Analysis of light-harvesting complex proteins reveals a nuclear-encoded phycobiliprotein in the alga. We uncover a complex set of carbohydrate-active enzymes, identify the genes required for the methylerythritol phosphate pathway of isoprenoid biosynthesis, and find evidence of sexual reproduction. Analysis of the compact, function-rich genome of P. purpureum suggests that ancestral lineages of red algae acted as mediators of horizontal gene transfer between prokaryotes and photosynthetic eukaryotes, thereby significantly enriching genomes across the tree of photosynthetic life. Red algae form one of the most ancient eukaryotic lineages, and have undergone multiple symbioses. Here, Price et al. report the first genome sequence for a mesophilic red alga, and reveal significant differences between these organisms and hyperthermopilic algae.
                  
                    
                  
                "/>

itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Institute of Molecular Physiology and Biotechnology of Plants (IMBIO), University of Bonn" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.10388.32, 0000 0001 2240 3300, Institute of Molecular Physiology and Biotechnology of Plants (IMBIO), University of Bonn, Bonn, 53115, Germany" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open href="#Aff11">11</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Institut National de la             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The limited knowledge we have about red algal genomes comes from the highly specialized extremophiles, Cyanidiophyceae. Here, we describe the first genome sequence from a mesophilic, unicellular red alga, <i>Porphyridium purpureum</i>. The 8,355 predicted genes in <i>P. purpureum</i>, hundreds of which are likely to be implicated in a history of horizontal gene transfer, reside in a genome of 19.7 Mbp with 235 spliceosomal introns. Analysis of light-harvesting complex proteins reveals a nuclear-encoded phycobiliprotein in the alga. We uncover a complex set of carbohydrate-active enzymes, identify the genes required for the methylerythritol phosphate pathway of isoprenoid biosynthesis, and find evidence of sexual reproduction. Analysis of the compact, function-rich genome of <i>P. purpureum</i> suggests that ancestral lineages of red algae acted as mediators of horizontal gene transfer between prokaryotes and photosynthetic eukaryotes, thereby significantly enriching genomes across the tree of photosynthetic life.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The red algae (Rhodophyta) form a monophyletic lineage comprising around 6,100 known species and 14,000 that are estimated to exist<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Guiry, M. D. How many species of algae are there? J. Phycol. 48, 1057–1063 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d57298e773">1</a></sup>. These taxa lack both flagella and centrioles during their life cycle<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Graham, L. D. &amp; Wilcox, L. W. Algae Prentice-Hall: USA, (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d57298e777">2</a></sup>. Rhodophytes include many unicellular mesophilic lineages, the extremophilic Cyanidiophyceae (for example, <i>Cyanidioschyzon</i> and <i>Galdieria</i>) that live in hot springs such as in Yellowstone National Park, and economically important seaweeds such as <i>Gracilaria</i> and <i>Pyropia</i> (including <i>Porphyra</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Blouin, N. A. Brodie, J. A. Grossman, A. C. Xu, P. &amp; Brawley, S. H. Porphyra: a marine crop shaped by stress. Trends Plant Sci. 16, 29–37 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d57298e797">3</a></sup> that are sources of agar and <i>nori</i>, respectively. Two aspects of red algal evolution are of central importance to understanding the evolution of eukaryotic phytoplankton. The first is their membership in the foundational lineage of photosynthetic eukaryotes, the supergroup Plantae (red algae, glaucophytes and green algae plus plants (also known as Archaeplastida)), whose ancestor putatively captured the canonical cyanobacterium-derived plastid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Chan, C. X. et al. Red and green algal monophyly and extensive gene sharing found in a rich repertoire of red algal genes. Curr. Biol. 21, 328–333 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d57298e804">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Price, D. C. et al. Cyanophora paradoxa genome elucidates origin of photosynthesis in algae and plants. Science. 335, 843–847 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d57298e807">5</a></sup>. The second is the subsequent spread of this organelle through secondary endosymbiosis to a diverse array of photosynthetic lineages collectively referred to as ‘chromalveolates’ (for example, diatoms, haptophytes and dinoflagellates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bhattacharya, D. Yoon, H. S. &amp; Hackett, J. D. Photosynthetic eukaryotes unite: endosymbiosis connects the dots. Bioessays 26, 50–60 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d57298e811">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Elias, M. &amp; Archibald, J. M. Sizing up the genomic footprint of endosymbiosis. Bioessays. 31, 1273–1279 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d57298e814">7</a></sup>) that are dominant marine primary producers. For instance, red alga-derived plastids in diatoms are responsible for about 25–50% of organic carbon fixed annually in the world’s oceans<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Falkowski, P. G. &amp; Raven, J. A. Aquatic photosynthesis Blackwell Science (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d57298e819">8</a></sup>. Secondary endosymbiosis also resulted in endosymbiotic gene transfer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Martin, W. &amp; Herrmann, R. G. Gene transfer from organelles to the nucleus: how much, what happens, and why? Plant. Physiol. 118, 9–17 (1998)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d57298e823">9</a></sup> (EGT) that relocated hundreds of former red algal genes to the nucleus of photosynthetic chromalveolates. Despite their central role in phytoplankton evolution, the genetic inventory of a unicellular lineage that may have been related to the putative donor of the ‘red plastid’ in chromalveolates is yet to be described. Existing rhodophyte complete genome sequences are from the thermoacidophiles <i>Cyanidioschyzon merolae</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Matsuzaki, M. et al. Genome sequence of the ultrasmall unicellular red alga Cyanidioschyzon merolae 10D. Nature 428, 653–657 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d57298e829">10</a></sup> and <i>Galdieria sulphuraria</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Schönknecht, G. et al. Gene transfer from bacteria and archaea facilitated evolution of an extremophilic eukaryote. Science. 339, 1207–1210 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d57298e835">11</a></sup> that have highly specialized and reduced genomes (for example, 16.5 Mbp with 5,331 protein-coding genes in <i>C. merolae</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Matsuzaki, M. et al. Genome sequence of the ultrasmall unicellular red alga Cyanidioschyzon merolae 10D. Nature 428, 653–657 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d57298e842">10</a></sup>) and from the red seaweed <i>Chondrus crispus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Collén, J. et al. Genome structure and metabolic features in the red seaweed Chondrus crispus shed light on evolution of the Archaeplastida. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 110, 5247–5252 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d57298e848">12</a></sup>.</p><p>Here, we analyse the draft genome assembly from the unicellular, mesophilic red alga <i>Porphyridium purpureum</i> CCMP 1328 (referred to as <i>P. cruentum</i> in this culture collection) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). This strain was isolated in 1957 from Eel Pond, Massachusetts and for this project was cultured in f/2 enriched seawater medium. We elucidate the role of horizontal gene transfer (HGT) in enriching the genome of <i>P. purpureum</i> and the extent of red algal gene sharing <i>via</i> EGT or HGT with chromalveolates and other taxa. We also analyse key aspects of red algal biology such as the evolution of proteins involved in light harvesting and metabolite transport and in starch, lipid and isoprenoid biosynthesis, and search for evidence of sexual reproduction in this species.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Analysis of the P. purpureum genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Analysis of the <i>P. purpureum</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2931/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2931/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2931_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2931/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2931_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="491" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Transmission electron microscopy image of a <i>P. purpureum</i> cell showing the central pyrenoid (Py), cell membrane (CM), starch granules (S) and plastid thylakoids (Th). (<b>b</b>) Percentage of single protein RAxML trees (raw numbers shown in the bars) that support the monophyly of <i>P. purpureum</i> (bootstrap ≥90%) solely with other Plantae members (exclusive), or in combination with non-Plantae taxa that interrupt this clade (non-exclusive). These latter groups of trees are primarily explained by red/green algal EGT into the nuclear genome of chromalveolates. For each of these algal lineages, the set of trees with different numbers of taxa (x) ≥4, ≥10, ≥20, ≥30 and ≥40 in a tree are shown. Each tree has ≥3 phyla. The Plantae-only groups are reds-greens-glaucophytes (RGGl) and reds-greens (RG).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2931/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Assembly and genome characteristics</h3><p>The <i>P. purpureum</i> nuclear genome, based on the total length of the assembled contigs, was estimated to be 19.7 Mbp in size. This genome is intron-poor with 235 spliceosomal introns present in the 8,355 gene models (that is, 2.8% of genes contain introns) predicted using mRNA-seq data (see Methods for details). In comparison, 0.5% of genes in <i>C. merolae</i> contain introns<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Nozaki, H. et al. A 100%-complete sequence reveals unusually simple genomic features in the hot-spring red alga Cyanidioschyzon merolae. BMC. Biol. 5, 28 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d57298e925">13</a></sup>. The mapping of 52 million genome sequence reads to the consensus assembly showed the presence of 26,383 single-nucleotide polymorphisms in contigs with average coverage &gt;10 × . Of these single-nucleotide polymorphisms, 94.6% were represented by two variants with an average representation of 57.5 and 42.4% for each variant, suggesting that the <i>P. purpureum</i> strain we sequenced was diploid. Previous studies have suggested both haploidy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Sivan, A. Thomas, J. C. Dubacq, J. P. van Moppes, D. &amp; Arad, S. Protoplast fusion and genetic complementation of pigment mutations in the red microalga Porphyridium sp. J. Phycol. 31, 167–172 (1995)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d57298e932">14</a></sup> and diploidy in this genus with the presence of 8–10 chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Schornstein, K. L. &amp; Scott, J. Ultrastructure of cell division in the unicellular red alga Porphyridium purpureum. Can. J. Bot. 60, 85–97 (1982)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d57298e937">15</a></sup>. Other analyses of the genome are shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Fig. S1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Tables S1 and S2</a>, and described in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Information</a>. The assembled genome and cDNA contigs, gene models, gene annotations, phylogenomic output and other material are available at <a href="http://cyanophora.rutgers.edu/porphyridium/">http://cyanophora.rutgers.edu/porphyridium/</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Analysis of gene transfer</h3><p>Phylogenomic analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Table S3</a>) resulted in 5,996 maximum likelihood protein trees (that is, 71.8% of the 8,355 total predicted proteins). At the stringent bootstrap threshold of ≥90% (or at ≥70 and ≥50%; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Fig. S2</a>; lists of sorted phylogenies in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Tables S4–S6</a>), about 20% of the trees supported the monophyly of red algae and other Plantae either by themselves (exclusive) or including other taxa (non-exclusive) that could have gained the Plantae gene through EGT or HGT (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>; and Methods section). At the bootstrap support threshold of ≥90%, we found 440–825 trees (7–14% of total trees; 5–10% of total 8,355 proteins) that show a strong association between red algae with one other lineage. As shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Fig. S2</a>, these numbers increase at the lower thresholds of ≥70% (766–1,310; 13–22% of total trees) and ≥50% (1,233–2,007; 21–33% of total trees). Of these trees (at bootstrap ≥90% in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>), ~40% showed sharing of red algal genes with different chromalveolate lineages either as nuclear genes or as cryptophyte nucleomorph-encoded<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Moore, C. E. &amp; Archibald, J. M. Nucleomorph genomes. Annu. Rev. Genet. 43, 251–264 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d57298e984">16</a></sup> homologues (for example, 60S ribosomal protein L10A; contig 2315.2, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Data 1</a>), and ~20% with prokaryote lineages. In an independent assessment of proteins in <i>C. merolae</i>, the corresponding proportion of prokaryote-associated red algal genes in this species is smaller (12%; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Fig. S3</a>). The majority of the <i>P. purpureum</i> trees (&gt;80% in the analysis based on a bootstrap threshold ≥90%) show an evolutionary history that is, however, too complicated (for example, poor resolution of clades or currently too few taxa in the trees) to interpret with confidence. Taking trees containing ≥3 phyla and ≥20 terminal taxa as an indicator (middle bar; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>), our results suggest that at least 453 genes (non-green portion; 5.4% of 8,355 proteins) in <i>P. purpureum</i> are impacted by E/HGT in their evolutionary history (773 genes at bootstrap ≥70%; 9.3% of 8,355). The complexity of these gene phylogenies is comparable to previous findings based on red algal transcriptome data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Chan, C. X. et al. Red and green algal monophyly and extensive gene sharing found in a rich repertoire of red algal genes. Curr. Biol. 21, 328–333 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1007">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Chan, C. X. et al. Porphyra (Bangiophyceae) transcriptomes provide insights into red algal development and metabolism. J. Phycol. 48, 1328–1342 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1010">17</a></sup>.</p><p>Using a stringent criterion (<i>P</i>≤0.05 and false discovery rate ≤0.10), we observed no significant biases in the putative functions of <i>P. purpureum</i> proteins that are associated with non-Plantae taxa (non-green portion in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>) in comparison with the annotated functions across the whole data set (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Fig. S4</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Tables S7–S9</a>). Such functional biases were observed in an earlier study based solely on transcriptome data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Chan, C. X. &amp; Bhattacharya, D. Non-random sharing of Plantae genes. Commun. Integr. Biol. 4, 361–363 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1033">18</a></sup>. This discrepancy is likely explained by EST assembly artifacts in the earlier analysis that resulted in partial or mis-assemblies that inflated the total number of genes and their relative representation in the database. Nevertheless, among genes with phylogenies that show clear evidence of a common origin in Plantae, we found significant over-representation of gene ontology (GO) terms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics 21, 3674–3676 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1037">19</a></sup> related to reproductive and cell development (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#Tab1">Table 1</a>), compared with the overall gene set (see Methods). This finding suggests that these genes are more likely to be vertically inherited within Plantae, or alternatively, that radiation/innovation of these genes occurred after the divergence of this supergoup.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of over-represented gene ontology terms.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms2931/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Given the complex evolutionary history of red algal genes found using phylogenomics combined with potential issues associated with the interpretation of results from automated pipelines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Deschamps, P. &amp; Moreira, D. Reevaluating the green contribution to diatom genomes. Genome Biol. Evol. 4, 683–688 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1497">20</a></sup>, we conducted a manual analysis of a contig in our assembly to test the results regarding E/HGT. Contig 2035 (of size 91,179 bp) has average coverage of 623 × and encodes 42 genes, all with transcriptome evidence that show a paucity of spliceosomal introns (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Fig. S5</a>). This <i>P. purpureum</i> genome region encodes proteins with a diversity of evolutionary histories. For example, one eukaryotic gene (contig 2035b.35) shows the expected monophyly of the lineages red algae, plants, Fungi and Metazoa in the eukaryotic tree of life (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>), whereas the neighbouring gene (contig 2035b.36), although also of eukaryotic provenance shows a reticulate history that involves Viridiplantae and chromalveolates (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). Other genes on contig 2035 are apparently of bacterial origin with one that is shared by glaucophytes and chromalveolates (contig 2035b.17, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>) and another that is shared only by red algae (contig 2035b.28, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#Fig2">Fig. 2d</a>). To gain a broader perspective on E/HGT, we also inspected phylogenies associated with all carbohydrate-active enzymes (CAZymes) identified in <i>P. purpureum</i> (see below for details). This revealed that of 107 CAZyme trees that could be interpreted with respect to prokaryotic or eukaryotic origin of the gene in <i>P. purpureum</i>, 41 genes (38%) had a prokaryotic provenance. Some of these genes were limited to red algae, whereas others were shared solely with Plantae and many (25 genes) had spread to chromalveolates.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Phylogenetic analysis of proteins on contig 2035b in P. purpureum."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Phylogenetic analysis of proteins on contig 2035b in <i>P. purpureum</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2931/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2931/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2931_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2931/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2931_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="592" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>These are all RAxML trees (WAG + Γ + I + F model) with the results of 100 bootstrap replicates shown on the branches. (<b>a</b>) Tree inferred from a squalene monooxygenase-like protein involved in sterol biosynthesis that shows the expected monophyly of red algae and of plants within the eukaryote tree of life. (<b>b</b>) Tree inferred from a tyrosine kinase/lipopolysaccharide-modifying enzyme that shows a complex phylogenetic relationship between red algae and chromalveolates. (<b>c</b>) Tree inferred from a glycosyltransferase of bacterial origin that is consistent with the monophyly of red algae and glaucophytes and a shared history of the gene in these taxa with chromalveolates, potentially <i>via</i> secondary EGT. (<b>d</b>) Tree inferred from an unknown protein in the aminotransferase superfamily that is present only in red algae and originated through HGT presumably from a proteobacterial source. The unit of branch length in each tree is the number of substitutions per site. The GenBank GI and Joint Genome Institute (JGI) accession codes (where available) are shown after each taxon name.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2931/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Light-harvesting complex proteins in <i>P. purpureum</i></h3><p>Cyanobacteria and algae use light-harvesting proteins that contain photopigments to channel the energy gained from photons toward the chlorophyll-containing reaction centres of photosystems PSI and PSII. In many cyanobacteria, the only light-harvesting antenna proteins are phycobilisomes. In green algae and plants, all light-harvesting proteins are members of the light-harvesting complex (LHC) family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Green, B. R. &amp; Durnford, D. G. The chlorophyll-carotenoid proteins of oxygenic photosynthesis. Annu. Rev. Plant Physiol. Plant Mol. Biol. 47, p685–714 (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1576">21</a></sup>. In contrast, red algae are an intermediate between the two because they contain phycobilisomes, primarily associated with PSII, while also containing LHC proteins associated with PSI. <i>P. purpureum</i> was the first organism to have its phycobilisomes isolated and some of the phycobiliproteins (PBPs) and the LHC proteins have been characterized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Grossman, A. R. Schaefer, M. R. Chiang, G. G. &amp; Collier, J. L. The phycobilisome, a light-harvesting complex responsive to environmental conditions. Microbiol. Rev. 57, 725–749 (1993)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1583">22</a></sup>. We identified seven LHC proteins in the <i>P. purpureum</i> genome (contigs 435.19, 491.7, 776.1, 2142.1, 2493.3, 3421.1 and 4406.7; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Fig. S6A</a>), which is consistent with previous analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Green, B. R. &amp; Durnford, D. G. The chlorophyll-carotenoid proteins of oxygenic photosynthesis. Annu. Rev. Plant Physiol. Plant Mol. Biol. 47, p685–714 (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1594">21</a></sup>. The sequence encoded on contig 491.7 was identical to the previously identified Lhcr1 from <i>P. purpureum</i> and the sequence encoded on contig 2493.3 was identical to Lhcr2 (refs <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Tan, S. Ducret, A. Aebersold, R. &amp; Gantt, E. Red algal LHC I genes have similarities with both Chl a/b- and a/c-binding proteins: a 21 kDa polypeptide encoded by LhcaR2 is one of the six LHC I polypeptides. Photosynth. Res. 53, 129–140 (1997)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1600">23</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Tan, S. Cunningham, F. X. &amp; Gantt, E. LhcaR1 of the red alga Porphyridium purpureum encodes a polypeptide of the LHCI complex with seven potential chlorophyll a-binding residues that are conserved in most LHCs. Plant Mol. Biol. 33, 157–167 (1997)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1603">24</a>). The other five LHC proteins were compared with the N-terminal fragment data from Tan <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Tan, S. Ducret, A. Aebersold, R. &amp; Gantt, E. Red algal LHC I genes have similarities with both Chl a/b- and a/c-binding proteins: a 21 kDa polypeptide encoded by LhcaR2 is one of the six LHC I polypeptides. Photosynth. Res. 53, 129–140 (1997)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1610">23</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#Tab2">Table 2</a>). Whereas these authors identified six unique protein bands, their sequencing results suggested that the 19.5 kDa band contained two unique proteins, which our results confirm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Tan, S. Ducret, A. Aebersold, R. &amp; Gantt, E. Red algal LHC I genes have similarities with both Chl a/b- and a/c-binding proteins: a 21 kDa polypeptide encoded by LhcaR2 is one of the six LHC I polypeptides. Photosynth. Res. 53, 129–140 (1997)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1618">23</a></sup>. Therefore, all seven <i>P. purpureum</i> LHC proteins are expressed. Phylogenetic analysis of the LHC proteins showed that, as expected, the <i>P. purpureum</i> sequences grouped with other red algae and chromalveolates (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Fig. S6A</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Comparison of molecular weights based on sequence prediction.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms2931/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Analysis of phycobilisome proteins showed <i>P. purpureum</i> contains alpha and beta subunits for phycoerythrin (PE) as well as several linker proteins (including L<sub>CM</sub>, L<sub>RC</sub>, L<sub>C</sub> and 4 γ<sup>PE</sup>) (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Data 1</a>). Surprisingly, we found a nuclear-encoded alpha-like PBP (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Fig. S6B</a>). As PBP bands are not well resolved in SDS–PAGE gels<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Redlinger, T. &amp; Gantt, E. Phycobilisome structure of Porphyridium purpureum: polypeptide composition. Plant Physiol. 68, 1375–1379 (1981)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1867">25</a></sup>, it is difficult to estimate from previous research the number of PBPs expressed in <i>P. purpureum</i>. The novel protein encoded on contig 2051.9 (252 aa in length) is associated in the tree with a number of cyanobacterial genes, one of which is a second α<sup>AP</sup> (allophycocyanin) from <i>Gloeobacter violaceus</i> (gi37520823). Whereas this second AP-α protein was identified from the sequencing of the <i>G. violaceus</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Mendoza-Hernández, G. Pérez-Gómez, B. Krogmann, D. W. Gutiérrez-Cirlos, E. B. &amp; Gómez-Lojero, C. Interactions of linker proteins with the phycobiliproteins in the phycobilisome substructures of Gloeobacter violaceus. Photosynth. Res. 106, 247–261 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1883">26</a></sup>, analysis of the phycobilisomes did not identify any homologues in <i>P. purpureum</i> to the gi37520823 gene product. Examination of the <i>G. violaceus</i> genome shows this gene to be in an apparent operon with a bilin biosynthesis protein (MpeU-like protein) and a hypothetical protein, suggesting that if expressed it likely has a role in light harvesting (results not shown). The transcriptome data from <i>P. purpureum</i> shows extensive expression (813 mapped reads) of the novel PBP-encoding gene and examination of the protein sequence reveals a ca. 60 amino acid N-terminal extension when compared with cyanobacterial homologues. This extension appears to specify plastid targeting (scores of TargetP=0.65, ChloroP=0.67, Predotar=0.26 and Wolfpsort=14.0) and contains a phenylalanine near the N-terminus (that is, in this case, MLMFVF) that is typical for Plantae plastid targeted proteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Price, D. C. et al. Cyanophora paradoxa genome elucidates origin of photosynthesis in algae and plants. Science. 335, 843–847 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1896">5</a></sup>. Although lacking introns (as do most <i>P. purpureum</i> genes), these data suggest the red algal gene is a nuclear-encoded plastid-targeted PBP. A phylogenetic tree that includes all of the PBPs and core-membrane linkers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Fig. S7</a>) demonstrates the evolutionary relationship between the alpha and beta subunits and AP, PC, PE and the core-membrane linker and is consistent with previous data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Apt, K. E. Collier, J. L. &amp; Grossman, A. R. Evolution of the phycobiliproteins. J. Mol. Biol. 248, 79–96 (1995)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1907">27</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Analysis of CAZymes and starch biosynthesis</h3><p>A total of 116 putative CAZymes and 40 additional proteins containing putative carbohydrate-binding modules were identified in <i>P. purpureum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#Tab3">Table 3</a>) using the CAZy annotation pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. The carbohydrate-active enZymes database (CAZy): an expert resource for glycogenomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, D233–D238 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1925">28</a></sup>. These genes have a complex phylogenetic history (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Tables S10–S12</a>). The genome of <i>P. purpureum</i> encodes 31 glycoside hydrolases (GH), 83 glycosyltransferases (GT) and two carbohydrate esterases, but similar to other unicellular rhodophytes and chlorophytes, lacks homologues of known polysaccharide lyases. <i>P. purpureum</i> encodes a larger number of GH and GT (114) genes when compared with <i>C. merolae</i> (82). Not surprisingly, the number of CAZy families is also 33% greater in <i>P. purpureum</i> (14 GH and 34 GT families) when compared with <i>C. merolae</i> (9 GH and 27 GT families), likely reflecting the complexity of <i>P. purpureum</i> cell-wall polysaccharides<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Arad, S. M. &amp; Levy-Ontman, O. Red microalgal cell-wall polysaccharides: biotechnological aspects. Curr. Opin. Biotechnol. 21, 358–364 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1951">29</a></sup>. In comparison with the highly complex pathways of starch metabolism in green algae (over 30 genes) and the more diverse pathway in glaucophytes (22 genes), <i>P. purpureum</i> displays an unusually simple enzyme network consisting of 19 genes with many critical biosynthetic steps represented by single enzymes. This includes a single soluble starch synthase (GT5) that must have the remarkable property of priming polysaccharide synthesis, seeding the formation of novel granules and elongating the different size classes of chains present on amylopectin. These functions require a minimum of four distinct types of enzymes in Viridiplantae and several analogous glucan synthases in glaucophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Ball, S. G. Colleoni, C. Cenci, U. Raj, J. N. &amp; Tirtiaux, C. The evolution of the glycogen and starch metabolism in eukaryotes gives molecular clues to understand the establishment of plastid endosymbiosis. J. Exp. Bot. 62, 1775–1801 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1959">30</a></sup>. More exceptional and seemingly not shared with other starch accumulating red algae is the likely presence of a single isoamylase gene (contig 3410.5). Distinct isoamylase-like GH13 glycoside hydrolases are known to be involved both in starch catabolism and in the synthesis of the amylopectin crystalline structure that distinguishes starch from glycogen. This dual function seems to require several isoamylase genes in all Plantae examined thus far<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Ball, S. G. Colleoni, C. Cenci, U. Raj, J. N. &amp; Tirtiaux, C. The evolution of the glycogen and starch metabolism in eukaryotes gives molecular clues to understand the establishment of plastid endosymbiosis. J. Exp. Bot. 62, 1775–1801 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1963">30</a></sup>. The presence of this single enzyme brings into question its involvement in both processes. However, absence of a second isoamylase correlates with the presence of three GH13 α-amylase candidate sequences. One of these (contig 4541.5) may have debranching activity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Choi, J. H. et al. Characterization of a novel debranching enzyme from Nostoc punctiforme possessing a high specificity for long branched chains. Biochem. Biophys. Res. Commun. 378, 224–229 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1967">31</a></sup>. This GH13 glycoside hydrolase is also found in some starch accumulating algae that apparently lack isoamylase genes and have a red algal plastid derived from secondary endosymbiosis. Of great interest here is the presence of another α-glucan synthase encoding gene (<i>GT5</i>), a granule-bound starch synthase that correlates with the presence of amylose in Porphyridiales (<i>Porphyridium</i> or <i>Rhodella</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Shimonaga, T. et al. Variation in storage α-polyglucans of red algae: amylose and semi-amylopectin types in Porphyridium and glycogen type in Cyanidium. Mar. Biotechnol. 9, 192–202 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d57298e1981">32</a></sup>, a unique feature of these unicellular algae when compared with other Rhodophyta. Other aspects of carbohydrate metabolism in <i>P. purpureum</i> are presented in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Information</a>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 CAZymes present in the <i>P. purpureum</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms2931/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Membrane transporters in <i>P. purpureum</i></h3><p>About 3.4% of the 8,355 predicted genes in the <i>P. purpureum</i> genome encode solute transporters, channels and pumps, which is similar to the corresponding numbers in green algae and land plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Data 2</a>). Strikingly, in contrast to the currently known genomes of land plants and green and red algae, <i>P. purpureum</i> contains a putative sodium–potassium ATPase (encoded on contig 2281.11). Sodium–potassium ATPases import K<sup>+</sup> into cells and Na<sup>+</sup> out of cells at the expense of ATP, thereby keeping intracellular sodium concentrations low and potassium concentrations high (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>). Thus, the pump contributes to maintaining cellular potassium and sodium homoeostasis in <i>P. purpureum</i>, which is exposed to high extracellular sodium concentrations in its environment. Furthermore, the sodium gradient across the plasma membrane that is set up by the sodium–potassium pump provides the driving force for secondary active sodium-coupled solute transporters. Indeed, the <i>P. purpureum</i> genome encodes several putatively sodium-driven transporters, such as a sodium:bicarbonate symporter (contig 2098.9), a sodium-dependent phosphate transport system (contig 2023.2), and a sodium:glucose cotransporter (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>), which is the first finding of a such a transporter in a photosynthetic organism. Interestingly, it has been recently demonstrated that <i>P. purpureum</i> can be grown to high cell densities in complete darkness with glucose as the sole carbon source<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Oh, S. H. et al. Lipid production in Porphyridium purpureum grown under different culture conditions. J. Biosci. Bioengineer. 108, 429–434 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2434">33</a></sup>. In addition to the sodium:glucose symporter, four putative sucrose transporters were found in <i>P. purpureum</i> (contigs 2016.15, 2025.48, 2077.1, 3677.2). Among photosynthetic eukaryotes, sucrose transporters until now have only been reported from land plants and from the extremophilic red alga <i>Galdieria sulphuraria</i> that is able to grow heterotrophically on a range of different carbon sources<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Barbier, G. et al. Comparative genomics of two closely related unicellular thermo-acidophilic red algae, Galdieria sulphuraria and Cyanidioschyzon merolae, reveals the molecular basis of the metabolic flexibility of Galdieria sulphuraria and significant differences in carbohydrate metabolism of both algae. Plant. Physiol. 137, 460–474 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2445">34</a></sup>. It is thus possible that <i>P. purpureum</i> in addition to the monosaccharide glucose is also able to exploit disaccharides, such as sucrose, for heterotrophic growth, likely by a proton-coupled co-transport mechanism. Alternatively, because genes encoding the enzymes required for sucrose catabolism are not detectable in the <i>P. purpureum</i> genome (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Methods</a>), these transporters might be involved in the transport of osmotically active solutes, such as trehalose or mannosylglycerate.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Analysis of a transporter in P. purpureum."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Analysis of a transporter in <i>P. purpureum.</i></b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2931/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2931/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2931_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2931/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2931_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="491" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Schematic image showing the putative sodium–potassium ATPase and sodium:glucose cotransporter identified in the <i>P. purpureum</i> genome data.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2931/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Cytochrome P450 genes in <i>P. purpureum</i></h3><p>Cytochrome P450 (CYP) is one of the largest gene families with 5,100 sequences annotated in plants, 1,461 in vertebrates, 2,137 in insects, 2,960 in fungi, 1,042 in bacteria, 27 in Archaea and two in viruses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Nelson, D. &amp; Werck-Reichhart, D. A P450-centric view of plant evolution. Plant J. 66, 194–211 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2496">35</a></sup>. Red algae are characterized by small genome sizes and therefore the species sequenced until now have a small number of <i>CYP</i> genes compared with Viridiplantae. The <i>P. purpureum</i> nuclear genome encodes 12 <i>CYP</i> genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Table S13</a>), whereas <i>C. merolae</i> and <i>G. sulphuraria</i> contain 5 and 7 <i>CYP</i> genes, respectively. The <i>P. purpureum</i> CYPs contain all the conserved P450 domains (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Table S14</a>), however, only three of these genes are orthologs of CYP clades already described: contig 3544.1 (CYP97), contig 2697.2 (CYP51) and contig 440.7 (CYP710). The remaining 9 <i>CYP</i> genes group with diverse eukaryotes in novel clades (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). Other aspects of CYP evolution in <i>P. purpureum</i> are presented in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Information</a>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Analysis of CYPs and sphingolipid metabolism in P. purpureum."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Analysis of CYPs and sphingolipid metabolism in <i>P. purpureum</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2931/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2931/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2931_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2931/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms2931_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="836" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Maximum likelihood (RAxML; LG + Γ + I + F model) tree of CYP sequences from <i>P. purpureum</i> and other eukaryotes. Support for internal branches was assessed using 100 bootstrap replicates. The major known CYP clans are indicated. (<b>b</b>) Putative sphingolipid synthesis pathway in <i>P. purpureum</i> deduced from analyses of vascular plants (for example, <i>Arabidopsis</i>). Modifications without candidate genes in the <i>P. purpureum</i> draft assembly are indicated in red. Cer, ceramide; GCS, glucosylceramide synthase; GIPC, glucosyl inositol phosphoryl ceramide; GlcCer, glucosylceramide; IPC, inositol phosphoryl ceramide; LCB, long chain base; and VLCFA, very long chain fatty acid.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2931/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Glycerolipid biosynthesis</h3><p>Membrane glycerolipid biosynthesis in <i>P. purpureum</i> follows the same path as is present in vascular plants and red algae such as <i>Porphyra</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Chan, C. X. et al. Porphyra (Bangiophyceae) transcriptomes provide insights into red algal development and metabolism. J. Phycol. 48, 1328–1342 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2597">17</a></sup> or <i>C. merolae</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Sato, N. &amp; Moriyama, T. Genomic and biochemical analysis of lipid biosynthesis in the unicellular rhodophyte Cyanidioschyzon merolae: lack of a plastidic desaturation pathway results in the coupled pathway of galactolipid synthesis. Eukaryot. Cell. 6, 1006–1017 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2603">36</a></sup> but with a few minor differences. In line with findings from other red algae, genes encoding the acetyl-CoA carboxylase subunits (<i>accA</i>, <i>accB</i> and <i>accD</i>) are encoded in the plastid, not the nuclear genome, as is the case for members of the Viridiplantae and their derived secondary endosymbionts. As also described for other red algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Chan, C. X. et al. Porphyra (Bangiophyceae) transcriptomes provide insights into red algal development and metabolism. J. Phycol. 48, 1328–1342 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2617">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Sato, N. &amp; Moriyama, T. Genomic and biochemical analysis of lipid biosynthesis in the unicellular rhodophyte Cyanidioschyzon merolae: lack of a plastidic desaturation pathway results in the coupled pathway of galactolipid synthesis. Eukaryot. Cell. 6, 1006–1017 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2620">36</a></sup>, <i>P. purpureum</i> lacks a plastid desaturation pathway including the gene encoding the soluble stearoyl acyl-ACP desaturase (<i>FAB2</i>) present in all members of the green lineage. This indicates that saturated C<sub>16</sub>– and C<sub>18</sub>– rather than monounsaturated fatty acids are exported from the plastid to the endoplasmic reticulum (ER). The first step in the biosynthesis of polyunsaturated fatty acids is catalysed by a Δ9-desaturase. The protein sequence encoded on contig 2306.6 contains an N-terminal cytochrome <i>b</i><sub><i>5</i></sub> domain, distantly related versions of which are present in red algae and fungi, but absent in plants. <i>P. purpureum</i> is able to synthesize eicosapentaenoic acid (EPA, 20:5<sup>Δ</sup><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Price, D. C. et al. Cyanophora paradoxa genome elucidates origin of photosynthesis in algae and plants. Science. 335, 843–847 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2645">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Falkowski, P. G. &amp; Raven, J. A. Aquatic photosynthesis Blackwell Science (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2648">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Schönknecht, G. et al. Gene transfer from bacteria and archaea facilitated evolution of an extremophilic eukaryote. Science. 339, 1207–1210 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2651">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Sivan, A. Thomas, J. C. Dubacq, J. P. van Moppes, D. &amp; Arad, S. Protoplast fusion and genetic complementation of pigment mutations in the red microalga Porphyridium sp. J. Phycol. 31, 167–172 (1995)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2654">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Chan, C. X. et al. Porphyra (Bangiophyceae) transcriptomes provide insights into red algal development and metabolism. J. Phycol. 48, 1328–1342 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2657">17</a></sup>). All required enzymes are encoded as single-copy genes in the nucleus and likely located in the ER with one exception. For the putative ω3-desaturase encoded on contig 2141.6 that catalyses the last step of EPA biosynthesis, a putative plastid transit peptide was identified and as expected, the N-terminus of the protein encoded phenylalanine residues (that is, MFAGF), indicating that ω3-desaturation takes place inside this organelle. This is in line with observations from labelling experiments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Khozin, I. Adlerstein, D. Bigongo, C. Heimer, Y. M. &amp; Cohen, Z. Elucidation of the biosynthesis of eicosapentaenoic acid in the microalga Porphyridium purpureum (II. Studies with radiolabeled precursors). Plant Physiol. 114, 223–230 (1997)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2661">37</a></sup> but contrasts with analyses of <i>Porphyra</i> EST data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Chan, C. X. et al. Porphyra (Bangiophyceae) transcriptomes provide insights into red algal development and metabolism. J. Phycol. 48, 1328–1342 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2669">17</a></sup>. In contrast to findings in other red algae, some genes involved in glycerolipid synthesis appear to be present in multiple copies. Three candidate genes encoding monogalactosyl diacylglycerol transferase (MGD) and two genes encoding digalactosyl diacylglycerol transferase (DGD) homologues required for galactolipid synthesis were identified. The model plant <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> also contains three MGD and two DGD orthologs. Whereas MGD1 and DGD1 are constitutively active at the chloroplast inner envelope, MGD2, MGD3 and DGD2 are present at the chloroplast outer envelope following phosphate deprivation. Under these conditions, phospholipids in ER membranes are replaced by galactolipids. This phenomenon is also known from other organisms including some bacteria, but remains to be biochemically validated in <i>P. purpureum</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Synthesis of sphingolipids</h3><p>Sphingolipids are ubiquitous lipids that are highly enriched in the plasma membrane<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Sperling, P. Franke, S. Lüthje, S. &amp; Heinz, E. Are glucocerebrosides the predominant sphingolipids in plant plasma membranes? Plant Physiol. Biochem. 43, 1031–1038 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2687">38</a></sup>. They are composed of an amino alcohol, the so-called sphingoid (long chain) base, amide-linked to a fatty acid. The long chain base can be further modified by the addition of complex sugar headgroups. Apart from their function as membrane building blocks, some sphingolipids also have signalling functions and are, for example, involved in cell cycle control and programmed cell death<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Zäuner, S. Ternes, P. &amp; Warnecke, D. Biosynthesis of sphingolipids in plants (and some of their functions). Adv. Exp. Med. Biol. 688, 249–263 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2691">39</a></sup>. We identified all genes necessary for the formation of complex glycosphingolipids in the nuclear genome of <i>P. purpureum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Table S15</a>). In addition to the findings in the <i>Porphyra</i> transcriptome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Chan, C. X. et al. Porphyra (Bangiophyceae) transcriptomes provide insights into red algal development and metabolism. J. Phycol. 48, 1328–1342 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2708">17</a></sup>, we also found a candidate for a long chain base-kinase (contig 4419.4). Unlike protein sequences from Viridiplantae, the candidate for the fatty acid α-hydroxylase (encoded on contig 522.16) contains an N-terminal cytochrome <i>b</i><sub><i>5</i></sub> domain similar to that in fungal orthologs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Possible evidence of sexual reproduction in <i>P. purpureum</i></h3><p>Multicellular red algae within the Florideophyceae are well known for their complex triphasic life cycles. Sexual reproduction is also known in Bangiophyceae such as <i>Porphyra</i> that has a haploid gametophyte and a diploid ‘conchocelis’ stage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Sahoo, D. Tang, X. &amp; Yarish, C. Porphyra–the economic seaweed as a new experimental system. Curr. Sci. 83, 1313–1316 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2732">40</a></sup>. Interestingly, one of the oldest multicellular eukaryotic fossils is believed to be the gametophytic stage of a Bangiophyceae, dating to rocks up to 1,200 million years old<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Butterfield, N. J. Knoll, A. H. &amp; Swett, K. A bangiophyte red alga from the Proterozoic of arctic Canada. Science 250, 104–107 (1990)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2736">41</a></sup>. Outside of the Florideophyceae and Bangiophyceae, however, very little is known about sexual reproduction in red algae, particularly for unicellular forms such as <i>P. purpureum</i>, with most accounts suggesting the lack of sex in this lineage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Gantt, E. &amp; Conti, S. F. The ultrastructure of Porphyridium purpureum. J. Cell Biol. 26, 365–381 (1965)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2743">42</a></sup>. This is surprising because sexual reproduction is believed to be an ancient feature of eukaryotes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Ramesh, M. A. Malik, S. -B. &amp; Logsdon, J. M. Jr. A phylogenomic inventory of meiotic genes: evidence for sex in Giardia and an early eukaryotic origin of meiosis. Curr. Biol. 15, 185–191 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2748">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Schurko, A. M. &amp; Logsdon, J. M. Jr. Using a meiosis detection toolkit to investigate ancient asexual “scandals” and the evolution of sex. Bioessays 30, 579–589 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2751">44</a></sup> and relatively few lineages have completely lost this ability. To address the possibility of ‘cryptic sex,’ we searched the <i>P. purpureum</i> genome for the eight meiosis-specific proteins SPO11, HOP1, HOP2, MND1, DMC1, MSH4, MSH5, REC8 and MER3 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Malik, S. -B. Pightling, A. W. Stefaniak, L. M. Schurko, A. M. &amp; Logsdon, J. M. Jr. An expanded inventory of conserved meiotic genes provides evidence for sex in Trichomonas vaginalis. PLoS ONE 3, e2879 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2757">45</a>). The presence of genes encoding a majority of these proteins is taken as evidence for meiosis and therefore sexual reproduction (for example, in <i>Giardia intestinalis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Ramesh, M. A. Malik, S. -B. &amp; Logsdon, J. M. Jr. A phylogenomic inventory of meiotic genes: evidence for sex in Giardia and an early eukaryotic origin of meiosis. Curr. Biol. 15, 185–191 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2763">43</a></sup>, <i>Trichomonas vaginalis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Malik, S. -B. Pightling, A. W. Stefaniak, L. M. Schurko, A. M. &amp; Logsdon, J. M. Jr. An expanded inventory of conserved meiotic genes provides evidence for sex in Trichomonas vaginalis. PLoS ONE 3, e2879 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2769">45</a></sup>).</p><p>Our search turned up evidence for eight of the targeted proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#Tab4">Table 4</a>) including two paralogs of SPO11 (SPO11-2 and SPO11-3). All trees are shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Figs S8–S15</a>. The presence of 8 out of 9 meiosis-specific proteins is consistent with (but does not prove) the maintenance of sexual reproduction in <i>P. purpureum</i>. Presence of all ‘toolkit’ proteins is not required for sexual reproduction. There are numerous examples of species known to be sexual that are missing one or more of these proteins. For example, <i>Drosophila melanogaster</i> lacks DMC1, HOP2, MND1, MSH4 and MSH5 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Schurko, A. M. &amp; Logsdon, J. M. Jr. Using a meiosis detection toolkit to investigate ancient asexual “scandals” and the evolution of sex. Bioessays 30, 579–589 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2787">44</a>). It would not be unusual if <i>P. purpureum</i> lacks REC8, because most protists, including known sexual species such as <i>Chlamydomonas</i>, also do not have this protein<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Schurko, A. M. &amp; Logsdon, J. M. Jr. Using a meiosis detection toolkit to investigate ancient asexual “scandals” and the evolution of sex. Bioessays 30, 579–589 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d57298e2798">44</a></sup>. It is likely that in lineages missing REC8, RAD21 (the mitotic paralog) functions as well in meiosis. In the case of <i>P. purpureum</i>, the two identified contigs could be the result of a <i>RAD21</i> gene duplication, whereby one of the paralogs has assumed a meiotic function.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-4"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab4" data-test="table-caption">Table 4 Identification of the meiotic toolkit genes in <i>P. purpureum</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms2931/tables/4"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><p>Analysis of the first genome sequence from a mesophilic, unicellular red alga turned up several surprises. First, the genome is tightly packed with coding regions and is intron poor, reminiscent of a bacterial genome. There are very few large gene families, suggesting that unicellular red algal extremophiles and mesophiles may have undergone a phase of genome reduction, perhaps in an extremophilic common ancestor of all Rhodophyta. It is also interesting (if not surprising) that hundreds (5.4–9.3%) of the 8,355 <i>P. purpureum</i> genes show evidence of a reticulated evolutionary history and are likely to be implicated in E/HGT, with many more gene with phylogenies that cannot be readily interpreted using existing data. These data shed light on recent debates about the role of HGT in microbial eukaryote genome evolution, in particular whether phagotrophic and parasitic lineages are more likely to capture foreign genes than strict photoautotrophs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Andersson, J. O. Horizontal gene transfer between microbial eukaryotes. Methods Mol. Biol. 532, 473–487 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3152">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Bhattacharya, D. et al. Single cell genome analysis supports a link between phagotrophy and primary plastid endosymbiosis. Sci. Rep 2, 356 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3155">47</a></sup>. In contrast to expectations, we demonstrate that anciently diverged relatives of the free-living, photosynthetic <i>P. purpureum</i> were mediators of HGT between prokaryotes and photosynthetic eukaryotes, <i>vis-à-vis</i> endosymbiosis. We have, however, no way of knowing for certain whether the red algal (or Plantae) ancestor was phagotrophic and therefore more prone to HGT, because evidence of HGT has also been described in non-phagotrophic lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Marcet-Houben, M. &amp; Gabaldón, T. Acquisition of prokaryotic genes by fungal genomes. Trends Genet. 26, 5–8 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3165">48</a></sup>. Regardless of the mechanism of ancient or more recent HGT, these data underline the fundamental importance of red algae to the evolution of eukaryotic plankton.
