Red algal plastids and red algal nuclear genes are now widespread in chlorophyll <i>c</i>-containing lineages such as diatoms, haptophytes and cryptophytes.</p><p>Other highlights of this genome include the finding of a nuclear-encoded PBP that apparently has a plastid function (that is, supported by the presence of a putative plastid-targeting signal), an unexpected diversity of <i>CYP</i> genes compared with green plants, and a simpler pathway for starch biosynthesis (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Data 3</a> for a list of the plastid encoded genes). As red algae have the longest fossil record known among eukaryotes (1.2 billion years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Butterfield, N. J. Knoll, A. H. &amp; Swett, K. A bangiophyte red alga from the Proterozoic of arctic Canada. Science 250, 104–107 (1990)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3182">41</a></sup>) and the lineage contains up to 14,000 species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Guiry, M. D. How many species of algae are there? J. Phycol. 48, 1057–1063 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3186">1</a></sup>, <i>P. purpureum</i> provides promise that a wealth of novel information awaits to be unearthed as additional genomes are completed from Rhodophyta.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Genome sequencing</h3><p>A total of 7.4 Gbp of <i>P. purpureum</i> CCMP 1328 paired end (150 × 150 bp) genome data generated using two flow cell lanes in the Illumina GAIIx were assembled with the CLC Genomics Workbench tools (<a href="http://www.clcbio.com/products/clc-genomics-workbench/">http://www.clcbio.com/products/clc-genomics-workbench/</a>) into 4,770 contigs with a N50 of 20,296 bp. The contigs had average nucleotide coverage of 376 × (median=56 × ) and totalled 19.7 Mbp, suggesting a genome size of ca. 20 Mbp. As with other genome studies, these estimates are subjected to further validation with better assembly of more sequence data. Thereafter, 4.1 Gbp of Illumina mRNA-seq data (150 bp x 150 bp reads) were used to train the ab-initio gene predictors (for details, see Price <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Price, D. C. et al. Cyanophora paradoxa genome elucidates origin of photosynthesis in algae and plants. Science. 335, 843–847 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3218">5</a></sup>), resulting in a set of 8,355 weighted consensus gene structures that were used for downstream analyses.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genome-wide analysis and phylogenomics</h3><p>Repeat elements were identified using RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>) against the Repbase repetitive DNA elements library (version 2012-04-18) and a <i>de novo</i> repeat library elements generated using Repeat modeller (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>). Duplicated genes from red algal genomes were identified using the method described in a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Gu, Z. Cavalcanti, A. Chen, F. C. Bouman, P. &amp; Li, W. H. Extent of gene duplication in the genomes of Drosophila, nematode and yeast. Mol. Biol. Evol. 19, 256–262 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3247">49</a></sup>, except that we required aligned regions to cover &gt;70% length of both proteins of the duplicated genes. The identity (<i>I</i>) cutoff for paralogous gene pairs was 30% if the total length of the aligned regions (<i>L</i>) was &gt;150 amino acids. When <i>L</i> was ≤150 amino acids, then the minimal <i>I</i> was found<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Rost, B. Twilight zone of protein sequence alignments. Protein. Eng. 12, 85–94 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3264">50</a></sup> using the formula <i>I</i>≥0.06+4.8<i>L</i><sup>−0.32(1+ exp(−<i>L</i>/1,000))</sup>. Paralogous gene pairs were clustered into gene families. A gene was assigned to a gene family if it was paralogous to one or more of its members. Phylogenomic analysis was done based on protein sequence alignments as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Chan, C. X. et al. Red and green algal monophyly and extensive gene sharing found in a rich repertoire of red algal genes. Curr. Biol. 21, 328–333 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3280">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Price, D. C. et al. Cyanophora paradoxa genome elucidates origin of photosynthesis in algae and plants. Science. 335, 843–847 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3283">5</a></sup> using MUSCLE 3.8.31 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3286">51</a>) (default settings) and RAxML 7.2.8 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3289">52</a>) (WAG + Γ model; 100 bootstrap replicates). Only trees that contained ≥3 phyla and a minimum number of taxa (<i>N</i>) ranging from 4 to 40 were considered, to minimize the impact of taxon sampling on this analysis. The screening for gene transfer is based on strongly supported clades consisting of rhodophyte(s) and one other phylum, as described in an earlier study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Chan, C. X. Reyes-Prieto, A. &amp; Bhattacharya, D. Red and green algal origin of diatom membrane transporters: insights into environmental adaptation and cell evolution. PLoS ONE 6, e29138 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3296">53</a></sup>, where prokaryote (or when unavailable, opisthokont) sequences were used as outgroup in rooting the trees. A phylogenetic tree was considered to show non-exclusive sharing of a Plantae gene when a strongly supported clade (at the defined bootstrap support threshold) was found that comprised ≥90% Plantae taxa with the remainder being non-Plantae (for example, stramenopiles). This type of gene history implies putative E/HGT between the Plantae and non-Plantae taxa, but nevertheless provides support for Plantae monophyly. We did not interpret these trees as evidence of E/HGT.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Analysis of gene functional biases</h3><p>Based on the annotated GO terms (<a href="http://geneontology.org/">http://geneontology.org/</a>) using Blast2GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics 21, 3674–3676 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3315">19</a></sup> (BLASTp E ≤10<sup>−5</sup>), we applied Fisher’s exact test to assess potential functional biases in a given set of <i>P. purpureum</i> genes (test set; for example, genes associated with Plantae or non-Plantae taxa) in comparison with the annotated terms across the overall 8,355 genes (the reference set; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Tables S7–S9</a>), with correction for multiple testing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Benjamini, Y. &amp; Hochberg, Y. Controlling the false discovery rate: a practical and powerful approach to multiple testing. J. R. Stat. Soc. B 57, 289–300 (1995)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3328">54</a></sup>. An over- or under-representation of a GO term in the test set is statistically significant when <i>P</i>≤0.05 and false discovery rate ≤0.10.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Identification and bioinformatic analysis of CAZymes</h3><p>All 8,355 putative ORFs encoded by the <i>P. purpureum</i> genome were submitted to analysis using the CAZy annotation pipeline in a two-step procedure of identification and annotation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. The carbohydrate-active enZymes database (CAZy): an expert resource for glycogenomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, D233–D238 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3346">28</a></sup>. Sequences were subjected to BLASTp analysis against a library composed of the full-length proteins of the CAZy database. The hits with an <i>e</i>-value better than 0.1 were then subjected to a modular annotation procedure, that combines BLASTp against libraries of catalytic and carbohydrate-binding modules and family-specific profile Hidden Markov models (for details, see Price <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Price, D. C. et al. Cyanophora paradoxa genome elucidates origin of photosynthesis in algae and plants. Science. 335, 843–847 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3356">5</a></sup>). The results were manually verified and completed with signal peptide, transmembrane and GPI predictions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Eisenhaber, B. Schneider, G. Wildpaner, M. &amp; Eisenhaber, F. A sensitive predictor for potential GPI lipid modification sites in fungal protein sequences and its application to genome-wide studies for Aspergillus nidulans, Candida albicans, Neurospora crassa, Saccharomyces cerevisiae and Schizosaccharomyces pombe. J. Mol. Biol 337, 243–253 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3361">55</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Käll, L. Krogh, A. &amp; Sonnhammer, E. L. A combined transmembrane topology and signal peptide prediction method. J. Mol. Biol. 338, 1027–1036 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3364">56</a></sup>. The fragmentary models and all models suspected of prediction errors were identified and flagged. Finally, a functional annotation step was carried out involving BLASTp comparisons against a library of modules derived from biochemically characterized enzymes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. The carbohydrate-active enZymes database (CAZy): an expert resource for glycogenomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, D233–D238 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3368">28</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Analysis of <i>CYP</i> genes</h3><p>The <i>P. purpureum</i> protein models were searched by BLASTp analysis with CYP protein sequences representing the CYP2, CYP3 and CYP4 animal clades<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Feyereisen, R. Insect CYP genes and P450 enzymes. InInsect Molecular Biology and Biochemistry Gilbert L. I. (ed.)236–316Elsevier: Amsterdam, (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3388">57</a></sup>, and the CYP51,CYP71, CYP72, CYP74, CYP85, CYP86, CYP97, CYP710, CYP711, CYP727 and CYP746 plant clades<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Nelson, D. &amp; Werck-Reichhart, D. A P450-centric view of plant evolution. Plant J. 66, 194–211 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3392">35</a></sup>. P450 sequences from other species, including those from <i>Bigelowiella natans</i> and <i>Guillardia theta</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Curtis, B. A. et al. Algal genomes reveal evolutionary mosaicism and the fate of nucleomorphs. Nature 492, 59–65 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3401">58</a></sup>, were identified in the same way and their CYP denomination were confirmed whenever possible using the standard P450 classification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Nelson, D. R. The cytochrome p450 homepage. Hum. Genomics 4, 59–65 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3406">59</a></sup>. Their CYP sequences accession codes and provenances are summarized in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2931#MOESM176">Supplementary Table S13</a>. The distantly related clade CYP727 and the divergent sequences CYP804A1 and CYP772A1 were excluded from the phylogenetic analysis. The CYP sequences were aligned using MUSCLE 3.8.31 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3412">51</a>) and a tree constructed using RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3416">52</a></sup> (WAG + Γ + I model; 100 bootstrap replicates).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Microscopy</h3><p>Transmission electron microscopy images were taken at the Center of Advanced Microscopy at Michigan State University, East Lansing, MI, USA on a JEOL100 CXII instrument (Japan Electron Optics Laboratories, Tokyo, Japan). For sample preparation, <i>P. purpureum</i> CCMP 1328 cells were processed as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Harris, E. H. The Chlamydomonas sourcebook: a comprehensive guide to biology and laboratory use Academic Press: San Diego, (1989)." href="/articles/ncomms2931#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d57298e3431">60</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec20" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec20-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec20">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec20-content"><p><b>Accession codes:</b> Coordinates for the draft <i>P. purpureum</i> genome and the Illumina mRNA-seq reads from this alga have been deposited at the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRA) with Project number BioProject ID# <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=PRJNA189757">PRJNA189757</a>, under the accession code <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRP018727">SRP018727</a>.</p><p><b>How to cite this article:</b> Bhattacharya, D. <i>et al</i>. Genome of the red alga <i>Porphyridium purpureum</i>. <i>Nat. Commun.</i> 4:1941 doi: 10.1038/ncomms2931 (2013).</p></div></div></section>

            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Bib1" data-title="References"><div class="c-article-section" id="Bib1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Bib1">References</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Bib1-content"><div data-container-section="references"><ol class="c-article-references"><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="MD. D.C.P. led the genome assembly and gene prediction, C.X.C. did the phylogenomic and gene ontology term analyses, H.Q. did the repeat and gene family analyses, N.R. Analysed the light-harvesting complex data, S.B., M.C.A., B.H., P.M.C. and A.K. studied carbohydrate-active enzymes and starch metabolism, S.Z. studied lipid biosynthesis genes, A.P.M.W. Analysed the membrane transporters, S.M. studied isoprenoid biosynthesis, F.H. studied cytochrome P450 genes and A.E. and M.M.P. studied the evolution of meiosis-specific genes. D.B. wrote the paper in collaboration with the co-authors. D.B. supervised the project and is responsible for the final manuscript version.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding author</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms2931/email/correspondent/c1/new">Debashish Bhattacharya</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Competing interests</h3>

                  Structural basis of energy transfer in Porphyridium purpureum phycobilisome
                </a>
              </h3>
              
                <ul data-test="author-list"
                    class="c-article-further-reading__authors-list js-list-authors-3 js-etal-collapsed" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"
                    id="authors-4">
                  <li><span
                        class="js-separator"></span><span>Jianfei Ma</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Xin You</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Shan Sun</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Xiaoxiao Wang</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Song Qin</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">&nbsp;&amp;&nbsp;</span><span>Sen-Fang Sui</span></li></ul>
              
              <p class="c-article-further-reading__journal-title" id="journal-name-4">
                <i>Nature</i>
                (2020)
              </p>
            </li>
          
            <li class="c-article-further-reading__item js-ref-item" id="further-reading-item-5">
              <h3 class="c-article-further-reading__title">
                <a class="print-link"
                   data-track="click"
                   data-track-action="view further reading article"
                   data-track-label="link:Diurnal.plant.tools: Comparative Transcriptomic and Co-expression Analyses of Diurnal Gene Expression of the Archaeplastida Kingdom"
                   href="https://doi.org/10.1093/pcp/pcz176"
                   id="title-link-5">
                  Diurnal.plant.tools: Comparative Transcriptomic and Co-expression Analyses of Diurnal Gene Expression of the Archaeplastida Kingdom
                </a>
              </h3>
              
                <ul data-test="author-list"
